## Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology, a comprehensive exploration of the fascinating world of igneous rocks and their processes. This chapter will delve into the intricate details of how these rocks are formed, their unique characteristics, and their role in the Earth's dynamic system.

Igneous petrology is a branch of petrology that focuses on the study of igneous rocks, which are rocks formed by the cooling and solidification of molten rock, or magma. These rocks are a significant part of the Earth's crust, and their study is crucial for understanding the geological history of our planet.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous rocks, including plutonic and volcanic rocks, and their formation processes. We will also delve into the factors that influence the composition and texture of these rocks, such as the type of magma, the cooling rate, and the pressure and temperature conditions.

We will also discuss the role of igneous rocks in the Earth's mantle and crust, and how they contribute to the formation of mountains, valleys, and other geological features. We will also touch upon the economic importance of these rocks, as they are the primary source of many valuable minerals and metals.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of igneous petrology, from the basic principles to the most complex processes. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for your studies and work.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of igneous petrology, where science meets nature in a spectacular display of power and beauty.




#### 6.5d Metamorphic P-T Paths: Constraints and Tectonic Implications

Metamorphic rocks provide valuable insights into the conditions under which they were formed, including temperature and pressure. These conditions, represented as pressure-temperature (P-T) paths, can be reconstructed from the mineral assemblages of metamorphic rocks. The P-T paths of metamorphic rocks can provide important constraints on the tectonic history of a region, including the timing and nature of metamorphism, as well as the processes that led to the formation of the metamorphic rocks.

##### Pressure-Temperature Paths

The pressure-temperature (P-T) path of a metamorphic rock is a graphical representation of the conditions under which the rock was formed. It is typically represented as a curve on a pressure-temperature diagram, with the pressure on the y-axis and the temperature on the x-axis. The P-T path of a metamorphic rock can be reconstructed from the mineral assemblage of the rock, which is influenced by the temperature and pressure at which the rock was formed.

The P-T path of a metamorphic rock can be divided into three main stages: the pre-peak stage, the peak stage, and the post-peak stage. During the pre-peak stage, the rock experiences increasing temperature and pressure. This stage is typically associated with the metamorphism of sedimentary rocks. During the peak stage, the rock experiences maximum temperature and pressure, which is typically associated with the formation of metamorphic minerals. During the post-peak stage, the rock experiences decreasing temperature and pressure, which is typically associated with the cooling and decompression of the rock.

##### Constraints on Tectonic History

The P-T paths of metamorphic rocks can provide important constraints on the tectonic history of a region. For example, the timing of metamorphism can be inferred from the P-T paths of metamorphic rocks. If the P-T paths of metamorphic rocks in a region show a consistent pattern, this can suggest a single phase of metamorphism. On the other hand, if the P-T paths show a variety of patterns, this can suggest multiple phases of metamorphism.

The nature of the metamorphism can also be inferred from the P-T paths of metamorphic rocks. For example, the presence of high-pressure minerals in a metamorphic rock can suggest deep burial or subduction, while the presence of low-pressure minerals can suggest shallow burial or exhumation.

##### Implications for Metamorphic Petrology

The study of metamorphic P-T paths has important implications for metamorphic petrology. By understanding the P-T paths of metamorphic rocks, we can better understand the processes that led to the formation of these rocks. This can help us to identify the sources of metamorphic rocks, as well as the conditions under which they were formed.

Furthermore, the study of metamorphic P-T paths can also help us to understand the evolution of metamorphic rocks. By tracing the P-T paths of metamorphic rocks, we can reconstruct the history of these rocks, including their formation, metamorphism, and exhumation. This can provide valuable insights into the tectonic history of a region, as well as the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over geological time.




#### 6.6a Greenschist Facies: Mineral Assemblages and Metamorphic Conditions

Greenschist facies is a type of metamorphic rock that is formed under low temperature and moderate pressure conditions. It is characterized by the presence of certain minerals, including chlorite, actinolite, and albite, which are indicative of the specific temperature and pressure conditions under which the rock was formed.

##### Mineral Assemblages

The mineral assemblage of a greenschist facies rock is primarily determined by the primary rock composition. For example, the equilibrium mineral assemblage of rocks subjected to greenschist facies conditions depends on whether the primary rock composition was a basalt, a sedimentary rock, or a metamorphic rock.

In greater detail, the greenschist facies is subdivided into subgreenschist, lower greenschist, and upper greenschist. Lower temperatures are transitional with and overlap the prehnite-pumpellyite facies, and higher temperatures overlap with and include sub-amphibolite facies.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical greenschist facies assemblages are called the "Barrovian Facies Sequence", and the lower-pressure "Abukuma Facies Series". Temperatures of approximately 500-700°C and depths of about 10-20 km are the typical envelope of greenschist facies rocks.

If burial continues along Barrovian Sequence metamorphic trajectories, greenschist facies gives rise to amphibolite facies assemblages, dominated by amphibole and eventually to granulite facies. Lower pressure, normally contact metamorphism produces albite-epidote hornfels while higher pressures at great depth produces eclogite.

##### Oceanic Basalts and Greenschist

Oceanic basalts in the vicinity of mid-ocean ridges typically exhibit sub-greenschist alteration. The greenstone belts of the various archean cratons are commonly altered to the greenschist facies. These ancient rocks are noted as host rocks for a variety of ore deposits in Australia, Namibia and Canada.

Greenschist-like rocks can also be formed under blueschist facies conditions if the original rock (protolith) contains enough magnesium. This explains the scarcity of blueschist preserved from before the Neoproterozoic Era 1000 Ma ago when the Earth's oceanic crust contained more magnesium than today's oceanic crust.

#### 6.6b Blueschist Facies: Mineral Assemblages and Metamorphic Conditions

Blueschist facies is another type of metamorphic rock that is formed under high pressure and low temperature conditions. Unlike greenschist facies, blueschist facies is characterized by the presence of certain minerals, including glaucophane, lawsonite, and omphacite, which are indicative of the specific temperature and pressure conditions under which the rock was formed.

##### Mineral Assemblages

The mineral assemblage of a blueschist facies rock is primarily determined by the primary rock composition. For example, the equilibrium mineral assemblage of rocks subjected to blueschist facies conditions depends on whether the primary rock composition was a basalt, a sedimentary rock, or a metamorphic rock.

In greater detail, the blueschist facies is subdivided into lower blueschist, middle blueschist, and upper blueschist. Lower temperatures are transitional with and overlap the greenschist facies, and higher temperatures overlap with and include the eclogite facies.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical blueschist facies assemblages are called the "Barrovian Facies Sequence", and the lower-pressure "Abukuma Facies Series". Temperatures of approximately 500-700°C and depths of about 10-20 km are the typical envelope of blueschist facies rocks.

If burial continues along Barrovian Sequence metamorphic trajectories, blueschist facies gives rise to amphibolite facies assemblages, dominated by amphibole and eventually to granulite facies. Lower pressure, normally contact metamorphism produces albite-epidote hornfels while higher pressures at great depth produces eclogite.

##### Oceanic Basalts and Blueschist

Oceanic basalts in the vicinity of mid-ocean ridges typically exhibit sub-greenschist alteration. The greenstone belts of the various archean cratons are commonly altered to the blueschist facies. These ancient rocks are noted as host rocks for a variety of ore deposits in Australia, Namibia and Canada.

Greenschist-like rocks can also be formed under blueschist facies conditions if the original rock (protolith) contains enough magnesium. This explains the scarcity of blueschist preserved from before the Neoproterozoic Era 1000 Ma ago when the Earth's oceanic crust contained more magnesium than today's oceanic crust.

#### 6.6c Eclogite Facies: Mineral Assemblages and Metamorphic Conditions

Eclogite facies is a high-pressure, low-temperature metamorphic rock that is characterized by the presence of certain minerals, including omphacite, rutile, and garnet. These minerals are indicative of the specific temperature and pressure conditions under which the rock was formed.

##### Mineral Assemblages

The mineral assemblage of an eclogite facies rock is primarily determined by the primary rock composition. For example, the equilibrium mineral assemblage of rocks subjected to eclogite facies conditions depends on whether the primary rock composition was a basalt, a sedimentary rock, or a metamorphic rock.

In greater detail, the eclogite facies is subdivided into lower eclogite, middle eclogite, and upper eclogite. Lower temperatures are transitional with and overlap the blueschist facies, and higher temperatures overlap with and include the granulite facies.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical eclogite facies assemblages are called the "Barrovian Facies Sequence", and the lower-pressure "Abukuma Facies Series". Temperatures of approximately 500-700°C and depths of about 10-20 km are the typical envelope of eclogite facies rocks.

If burial continues along Barrovian Sequence metamorphic trajectories, eclogite facies gives rise to amphibolite facies assemblages, dominated by amphibole and eventually to granulite facies. Lower pressure, normally contact metamorphism produces albite-epidote hornfels while higher pressures at great depth produces eclogite.

##### Oceanic Basalts and Eclogite

Oceanic basalts in the vicinity of mid-ocean ridges typically exhibit sub-greenschist alteration. The greenstone belts of the various archean cratons are commonly altered to the eclogite facies. These ancient rocks are noted as host rocks for a variety of ore deposits in Australia, Namibia and Canada.

Greenschist-like rocks can also be formed under eclogite facies conditions if the original rock (protolith) contains enough magnesium. This explains the scarcity of eclogite preserved from before the Neoproterozoic Era 1000 Ma ago when the Earth's oceanic crust contained more magnesium than today's oceanic crust.

As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's rocks and processes, we can look forward to many more exciting developments in the field of metamorphic petrology.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology, a crucial component of our comprehensive guide on petrology. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of igneous rocks, their formation, and the processes that shape them. 

Igneous petrology is a specialized field within petrology that focuses on the study of igneous rocks. These rocks are formed from the solidification of molten rock, or magma, which is a common occurrence in the Earth's mantle and crust. The study of igneous petrology is essential in understanding the geological history of the Earth, as well as predicting volcanic activity and mineral deposits.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous rocks, their characteristics, and how they are formed. We will also delve into the processes that govern the formation of these rocks, including the role of pressure, temperature, and the composition of the magma. 

We will also discuss the importance of igneous petrology in various fields, including geology, geophysics, and mineral exploration. The knowledge gained from studying igneous petrology can provide valuable insights into the Earth's structure and evolution, as well as the potential for mineral deposits.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of igneous petrology, from the basic principles to the advanced theories. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your journey to understand the complex world of igneous rocks.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of igneous petrology, where science meets nature in a spectacular display of geological processes.




#### 6.6b Blueschist Facies: Mineral Assemblages and Metamorphic Processes

The blueschist facies is a high-pressure, low-temperature metamorphic facies that is characterized by the presence of blue minerals such as glaucophane and lawsonite. This facies is typically found in subduction zones, where oceanic crust is being consumed by a plate margin. The blueschist facies is a result of the intense pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock as it descends into the mantle.

##### Mineral Assemblages

The mineral assemblage of a blueschist facies rock is primarily determined by the primary rock composition and the specific conditions of the subduction zone. However, some common minerals found in blueschists include glaucophane, lawsonite, chlorite, and albite. These minerals are indicative of the specific temperature and pressure conditions under which the rock was formed.

##### Metamorphic Processes

The metamorphic processes that create blueschist facies assemblages are complex and involve a combination of physical and chemical processes. The high pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock in the subduction zone cause it to undergo plastic deformation, resulting in the formation of schistose textures. Additionally, the high pressures and low temperatures cause the rock to undergo hydration, resulting in the formation of minerals such as glaucophane and lawsonite.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical blueschist facies assemblages are characterized by high pressures (greater than 1 GPa) and low temperatures (less than 500°C). These conditions are typically found in the upper mantle, where the rock is being consumed by a subduction zone. The specific conditions can vary depending on the composition of the primary rock and the specific characteristics of the subduction zone.

##### Oceanic Basalts and Blueschist

Oceanic basalts in the vicinity of subduction zones can also exhibit blueschist facies alteration. This is due to the intense pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock as it descends into the mantle. The formation of blueschist facies assemblages in these rocks can provide valuable insights into the processes occurring in subduction zones.

#### 6.6c Eclogites: Composition, Formation, and Metamorphic Processes

Eclogites are a type of high-pressure, low-temperature metamorphic rock that is typically found in the upper mantle. They are characterized by their dark green to black color and their high density. Eclogites are composed primarily of olivine, omphacite, and rutile, with minor amounts of other minerals such as chlorite and albite.

##### Composition

The composition of eclogites is primarily determined by the primary rock composition and the specific conditions of the upper mantle. However, some common minerals found in eclogites include olivine, omphacite, and rutile. These minerals are indicative of the specific temperature and pressure conditions under which the rock was formed.

##### Formation

Eclogites are typically formed in the upper mantle, where they are subjected to high pressures and low temperatures. The high pressures and low temperatures cause the rock to undergo plastic deformation, resulting in the formation of schistose textures. Additionally, the high pressures and low temperatures cause the rock to undergo hydration, resulting in the formation of minerals such as omphacite and rutile.

##### Metamorphic Processes

The metamorphic processes that create eclogite assemblages are complex and involve a combination of physical and chemical processes. The high pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock in the upper mantle cause it to undergo plastic deformation, resulting in the formation of schistose textures. Additionally, the high pressures and low temperatures cause the rock to undergo hydration, resulting in the formation of minerals such as omphacite and rutile.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical eclogite assemblages are characterized by high pressures (greater than 1 GPa) and low temperatures (less than 500°C). These conditions are typically found in the upper mantle, where the rock is being consumed by a subduction zone. The specific conditions can vary depending on the composition of the primary rock and the specific characteristics of the subduction zone.

##### Oceanic Basalts and Eclogites

Oceanic basalts in the vicinity of subduction zones can also exhibit eclogite facies alteration. This is due to the intense pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock as it descends into the mantle. The formation of eclogites in these rocks can provide valuable insights into the processes occurring in subduction zones.

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of metamorphic petrology, exploring the processes and rocks that are formed under extreme conditions of temperature and pressure. We have learned about the different types of metamorphic rocks, including greenschists, blueschists, and eclogites, and how they are formed. We have also examined the role of metamorphic processes in the evolution of the Earth's crust and mantle.

The study of metamorphic petrology is crucial in understanding the dynamic nature of the Earth's interior. It provides insights into the processes that shape our planet, from the formation of mountains to the recycling of oceanic crust. By studying metamorphic rocks, we can gain a better understanding of the conditions that exist deep within the Earth, and how these conditions can change over time.

In conclusion, metamorphic petrology is a complex and fascinating field that offers a wealth of opportunities for further study. By combining the principles of geology, chemistry, and physics, we can continue to unravel the mysteries of metamorphic rocks and their processes.

## Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 7: Igneous Petrology, a comprehensive exploration of the fascinating world of igneous rocks and their processes. This chapter will delve into the intricate details of igneous petrology, a branch of petrology that focuses on the study of igneous rocks, their formation, and the processes that shape them.

Igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, or magma, beneath the Earth's surface. They are a fundamental part of the Earth's crust and play a crucial role in shaping the planet's surface. Understanding the processes that form igneous rocks is essential for comprehending the dynamics of the Earth's interior and its evolution over time.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous rocks, their characteristics, and the conditions under which they are formed. We will also delve into the processes that drive the movement of magma, the formation of different types of igneous rocks, and the factors that influence these processes.

We will also discuss the role of igneous rocks in the Earth's history, their use in various industries, and the ongoing research in this field. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of igneous petrology, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to explore this fascinating field further.

#### 6.6c Eclogite Facies: Mineral Assemblages and Petrologic Significance

The eclogite facies is a high-pressure, high-temperature metamorphic facies that is characterized by the presence of dense, dark minerals such as omphacite and rutile. This facies is typically found in the deep mantle, where the extreme pressures and temperatures cause the rock to undergo extensive melting and recrystallization.

##### Mineral Assemblages

The mineral assemblage of an eclogite facies rock is primarily determined by the specific conditions of the mantle. However, some common minerals found in eclogites include omphacite, rutile, and majorite. These minerals are indicative of the extreme pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock in the deep mantle.

##### Petrologic Significance

The petrologic significance of the eclogite facies is immense. The extreme conditions under which these rocks form provide valuable insights into the processes occurring in the deep mantle. For instance, the presence of omphacite and rutile can indicate the depth at which the rock was formed, as these minerals are stable at high pressures and temperatures. Additionally, the presence of majorite can provide information about the composition of the mantle, as this mineral is typically found in peridotite-rich rocks.

##### Metamorphic Processes

The metamorphic processes that create eclogite facies assemblages are complex and involve a combination of physical and chemical processes. The high pressures and temperatures experienced by the rock in the deep mantle cause it to undergo extensive melting and recrystallization, resulting in the formation of dense, dark minerals. Additionally, the high pressures and temperatures can also cause the rock to undergo plastic deformation, resulting in the formation of schistose textures.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions which create typical eclogite facies assemblages are characterized by high pressures (greater than 1 GPa) and high temperatures (greater than 1000°C). These conditions are typically found in the deep mantle, where the rock is being consumed by a subduction zone. The specific conditions can vary depending on the composition of the mantle and the specific characteristics of the subduction zone.




#### 6.6d High-Pressure Metamorphism: Subduction Zones and Continental Collision

High-pressure metamorphism is a result of the extreme conditions found in subduction zones and continental collisions. These processes are responsible for the formation of greenschists, blueschists, and eclogites, which are important rocks in the study of metamorphic petrology.

##### Subduction Zones

Subduction zones are regions where one tectonic plate is being forced beneath another plate into the mantle. This process is driven by plate tectonics and is responsible for the formation of many of the world's mountain ranges. The high pressures and temperatures experienced in subduction zones can lead to the formation of greenschists and blueschists, which are characterized by the presence of chlorite and albite, respectively.

##### Continental Collision

Continental collision occurs when two continental plates collide, resulting in the formation of a mountain range. This process is responsible for the formation of the Himalayas, which are home to some of the highest peaks in the world. The extreme pressures and temperatures experienced during continental collision can lead to the formation of eclogites, which are characterized by the presence of dense, dark minerals such as omphacite and rutile.

##### Metamorphic Processes

The metamorphic processes that occur in subduction zones and continental collisions are complex and involve a combination of physical and chemical processes. The high pressures and temperatures experienced in these regions cause the rocks to undergo extensive melting and recrystallization, resulting in the formation of new minerals and textures. The specific conditions of the environment, such as the composition of the rocks and the presence of fluids, can also influence the type of metamorphism that occurs.

##### Metamorphic Conditions

The metamorphic conditions in subduction zones and continental collisions are characterized by high pressures (greater than 1 GPa) and high temperatures (greater than 600°C). These conditions can lead to the formation of a variety of metamorphic rocks, including greenschists, blueschists, and eclogites. The specific conditions can vary depending on the location and the composition of the rocks, but they are generally characterized by high pressures and temperatures.
